/>.com</a></span></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>Neil Richard MacKinnon Gaiman</b><sup id="cite_ref-LexMinMan20110114_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LexMinMan20110114-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/\xcb\x88/: primary stress follows">\xcb\x88</span><span title="/\xc9\xa1/: &#39;g&#39; in &#39;guy&#39;">\xc9\xa1</span><span title="/e\xc9\xaa/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;face&#39;">e\xc9\xaa</span><span title="&#39;m&#39; in &#39;my&#39;">m</span><span title="/\xc9\x99n/: &#39;on&#39; in &#39;button&#39;">\xc9\x99n</span></span>/</a></span></span>;<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> born <b>Neil Richard Gaiman</b>,<sup id="cite_ref-LexMinMan20110114_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LexMinMan20110114-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> 10 November 1960)<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> is an English author of short fiction, novels, comic books, <a href="/wiki/Graphic_novel" title="Graphic novel">graphic novels</a>, audio theatre, and films. His  works include the comic book series <i><a href="/wiki/The_Sandman_(Vertigo)" title="The Sandman (Vertigo)">The Sandman</a></i> and novels <i><a href="/wiki/Stardust_(novel)" title="Stardust (novel)">Stardust</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/American_Gods" title="American Gods">American Gods</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Coraline" title="Coraline">Coraline</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Graveyard_Book" title="The Graveyard Book">The Graveyard Book</a></i>. He has won numerous awards, including the <a href="/wiki/Hugo_Award" title="Hugo Award">Hugo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nebula_Award" title="Nebula Award">Nebula</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Bram_Stoker_Award" title="Bram Stoker Award">Bram Stoker</a> awards, as well as the <a href="/wiki/Newbery_Medal" title="Newbery Medal">Newbery</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carnegie_Medal_(literary_award)" title="Carnegie Medal (literary award)">Carnegie</a> medals. He is the first author to win both the Newbery and the Carnegie medals for the same work, <i>The Graveyard Book</i> (2008).<sup id="cite_ref-flood_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-flood-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-bbc_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> In 2013, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Ocean_at_the_End_of_the_Lane" title="The Ocean at the End of the Lane">The Ocean at the End of the Lane</a></i> was voted Book of the Year in the British <a href="/wiki/Specsavers_National_Book_Awards" class="mw-redirect" title="Specsavers National Book Awards">National Book Awards</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-BOTY_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BOTY-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">Literary allusions</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-17"><a href="#Selected_awards_and_honours"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Selected awards and honours</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-18"><a href="#Bibliography"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Bibliography</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-19"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-20"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life">Early life</span></h2>\n<p>Neil Gaiman\'s family is of Polish-Jewish and other Eastern European-Jewish origins;<sup id="cite_ref-princeinterview_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-princeinterview-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> his great-grandfather emigrated from <a href="/wiki/Antwerp" title="Antwerp">Antwerp</a>, Belgium, to the UK before 1914<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> and his grandfather eventually settled in the south of England in the <a href="/wiki/Hampshire" title="Hampshire">Hampshire</a> city of <a href="/wiki/Portsmouth" title="Portsmouth">Portsmouth</a> and established a chain of grocery stores. His father, <a href="/wiki/David_Gaiman" title="David Gaiman">David Bernard Gaiman</a>, worked in the same chain of stores;<sup id="cite_ref-argus_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-argus-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> his mother, Sheila Gaiman (<i>n\xc3\xa9e</i> Goldman), was a pharmacist. He has two younger sisters, Claire and Lizzy.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> After living for a period in the nearby town of <a href="/wiki/Portchester" title="Portchester">Portchester</a>, Hampshire, where Neil was born in 1960, the Gaimans moved in 1965 to the <a href="/wiki/West_Sussex" title="West Sussex">West Sussex</a> town of <a href="/wiki/East_Grinstead" title="East Grinstead">East Grinstead</a> where his parents studied <a href="/wiki/Dianetics" title="Dianetics">Dianetics</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Saint_Hill_Manor" title="Saint Hill Manor">Scientology centre</a> in the town; one of Gaiman\'s sisters works for the Church of Scientology in Los Angeles. His other sister, Lizzy Calcioli, has said, "Most of our social activities were involved with Scientology or our Jewish family. It would get very confusing when people would ask my religion as a kid. I\xe2\x80\x99d say, \'I\xe2\x80\x99m a Jewish Scientologist.<span style="padding-right:.2em;">\'</span>" Gaiman says that he is not a Scientologist, and that like Judaism, Scientology is his family\'s religion.<sup id="cite_ref-Goodyear_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Goodyear-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> About his personal views, Gaiman has stated, "I think we can say that God exists in the DC Universe. I would not stand up and beat the drum for the existence of God in this universe. I don\'t know, I think there\'s probably a 50/50 chance. It doesn\'t really matter to me."<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Gaiman was able to read at the age of four. He said, "I was a reader. I loved reading. Reading things gave me pleasure. I was very good at most subjects in school, not because I had any particular aptitude in them, but because normally on the first day of school they\'d hand out schoolbooks, and I\'d read them\xe2\x80\x94which would mean that I\'d know what was coming up, because I\'d read it."<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> When he was about ten years old, he read his way through the works of <a href="/wiki/Dennis_Wheatley" title="Dennis Wheatley">Dennis Wheatley</a>, where especially <i>The Ka of Gifford Hillary</i> and <i>The Haunting of Toby Jugg</i> made an impact on him.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> One work that made a particular impression on him was <a href="/wiki/J._R._R._Tolkien" title="J. R. R. Tolkien">J. R. R. Tolkien</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Lord_of_the_Rings" title="The Lord of the Rings">The Lord of the Rings</a></i> from his school library, although it only had the first two volumes of the novel. He consistently took them out and read them. He would later win the school English prize and the school reading prize, enabling him to finally acquire the third volume.<sup id="cite_ref-Abbey_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abbey-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>For his seventh birthday, Gaiman received <a href="/wiki/C._S._Lewis" title="C. S. Lewis">C. S. Lewis</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Chronicles_of_Narnia" title="The Chronicles of Narnia">The Chronicles of Narnia</a></i> series. He later recalled that "I admired his use of parenthetical statements to the reader, where he would just talk to you ... I\'d think, \'Oh, my gosh, that is so cool! I want to do that! When I become an author, I want to be able to do things in parentheses.\' I liked the power of putting things in brackets."<sup id="cite_ref-Abbey_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abbey-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> <i>Narnia</i> also introduced him to literary awards, specifically the 1956 <a href="/wiki/Carnegie_Medal_(literary_award)" title="Carnegie Medal (literary award)">Carnegie Medal</a> won by the concluding volume. When Gaiman won the 2010 Medal himself, the press reported him recalling, "it had to be the most important literary award there ever was"<sup id="cite_ref-bbc_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> and observing, "if you can make yourself aged seven happy, you\'re really doing well \xe2\x80\x93 it\'s like writing a letter to yourself aged seven."<sup id="cite_ref-flood_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-flood-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ardingly_College,_Sussex.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Ardingly_College%2C_Sussex.jpg/220px-Ardingly_College%2C_Sussex.jpg" width="220" height="138" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Ardingly_College%2C_Sussex.jpg/330px-Ardingly_College%2C_Sussex.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Ardingly_College%2C_Sussex.jpg/440px-Ardingly_College%2C_Sussex.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4000" data-file-height="2516" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ardingly_College,_Sussex.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Gaiman attended <a href="/wiki/Ardingly_College" title="Ardingly College">Ardingly College</a> in <a href="/wiki/Ardingly" title="Ardingly">Ardingly</a>, West Sussex</div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Lewis_Carroll" title="Lewis Carroll">Lewis Carroll</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Alice%27s_Adventures_in_Wonderland" title="Alice&#39;s Adventures in Wonderland">Alice\'s Adventures in Wonderland</a></i> was another childhood favourite, and "a favourite forever. Alice was default reading to the point where I knew it by heart."<sup id="cite_ref-Abbey_18-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abbey-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> He also enjoyed <a href="/wiki/Batman" title="Batman">Batman</a> comics as a child.<sup id="cite_ref-Abbey_18-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abbey-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Gaiman was educated at several <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a> schools, including Fonthill School in East Grinstead,<sup id="cite_ref-egnet_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-egnet-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ardingly_College" title="Ardingly College">Ardingly College</a> (1970\xe2\x80\x9374), and <a href="/wiki/Whitgift_School" title="Whitgift School">Whitgift School</a> in <a href="/wiki/Croydon" title="Croydon">Croydon</a> (1974\xe2\x80\x9377).<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> His father\'s position as a public relations official of the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Scientology" title="Church of Scientology">Church of Scientology</a> was the cause of the seven-year-old Gaiman being blocked from entering a boys\' school, forcing him to remain at the school that he had previously been attending.<sup id="cite_ref-Goodyear_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Goodyear-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> He lived in East Grinstead for many years, from 1965 to 1980 and again from 1984 to 1987.<sup id="cite_ref-egnet_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-egnet-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> He met his first wife, Mary McGrath, while she was studying Scientology and living in a house in East Grinstead that was owned by his father. The couple were married in 1985 after having their first child, Michael.<sup id="cite_ref-Goodyear_14-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Goodyear-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Career">Career</span></h2>\n<h3><span id="Journalism.2C_early_writings.2C_and_literary_influences"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Journalism,_early_writings,_and_literary_influences">Journalism, early writings, and literary influences</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><div id="mwe_player_4" class="PopUpMediaTransform" style="width:220px;" videopayload="&lt;div class=&quot;mediaContainer&quot; data-height=&quot;360&quot; data-bandwidth=&quot;563296&quot; data-framerate=&quot;1000&quot;/&gt;&lt;/video&gt;&lt;/div&gt;"><img alt="File:Neil Gaiman - Join the Open Rights Group.webm" style="width:220px;height:124px" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Neil_Gaiman_-_Join_the_Open_Rights_Group.webm/220px--Neil_Gaiman_-_Join_the_Open_Rights_Group.webm.jpg" /><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a8/Neil_Gaiman_-_Join_the_Open_Rights_Group.webm" title="Play media" target="new"><span class="play-btn-large"><span class="mw-tmh-playtext">Play media</span></span></a></div>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Neil_Gaiman_-_Join_the_Open_Rights_Group.webm" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Gaiman, an <a href="/wiki/Open_Rights_Group" title="Open Rights Group">Open Rights Group</a> patron, speaks about his concerns for creators\' rights.</div></div></div>\n<p>As a child and a teenager, Gaiman read the works of <a href="/wiki/C._S._Lewis" title="C. S. Lewis">C. S. Lewis</a>, <a href="/wiki/J._R._R._Tolkien" title="J. R. R. Tolkien">J. R. R. Tolkien</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lewis_Carroll" title="Lewis Carroll">Lewis Carroll</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mary_Shelley" title="Mary Shelley">Mary Shelley</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rudyard_Kipling" title="Rudyard Kipling">Rudyard Kipling</a>, <a href="/wiki/Edgar_Allan_Poe" title="Edgar Allan Poe">Edgar Allan Poe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Michael_Moorcock" title="Michael Moorcock">Michael Moorcock</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alan_Moore" title="Alan Moore">Alan Moore</a>, <a href="/wiki/Steve_Ditko" title="Steve Ditko">Steve Ditko</a><sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>, <a href="/wiki/Will_Eisner" title="Will Eisner">Will Eisner</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ursula_K._Le_Guin" title="Ursula K. Le Guin">Ursula K. Le Guin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Harlan_Ellison" title="Harlan Ellison">Harlan Ellison</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lord_Dunsany" class="mw-redirect" title="Lord Dunsany">Lord Dunsany</a> and <a href="/wiki/G._K._Chesterton" title="G. K. Chesterton">G. K. Chesterton</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Abbey_18-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abbey-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-MOR_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MOR-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> When he was 19\xe2\x80\x9320 years old, he contacted his favourite science fiction writer, <a href="/wiki/R._A._Lafferty" title="R. A. Lafferty">R. A. Lafferty</a>, whom he discovered when he was nine, and asked for advice on becoming an author along with a Lafferty <a href="/wiki/Pastiche" title="Pastiche">pastiche</a> he had written. The writer sent Gaiman an encouraging and informative letter back, along with literary advice.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Gaiman has said <a href="/wiki/Roger_Zelazny" title="Roger Zelazny">Roger Zelazny</a> was the author who influenced him the most,<sup id="cite_ref-Zelazny_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zelazny-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> with this influence particularly seen in Gaiman\'s literary style and the topics he writes about.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> Other authors Gaiman says "furnished the inside of my mind and set me to writing" include Moorcock, Ellison, <a href="/wiki/Samuel_R._Delany" title="Samuel R. Delany">Samuel R. Delany</a>, <a href="/wiki/Angela_Carter" title="Angela Carter">Angela Carter</a>, Lafferty and Le Guin.<sup id="cite_ref-Zelazny_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zelazny-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the early 1980s, Gaiman pursued journalism, conducting interviews and writing book reviews, as a means to learn about the world and to make connections that he hoped would later assist him in getting published.<sup id="cite_ref-Abbey_18-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abbey-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> He wrote and reviewed extensively for the British Fantasy Society.<sup id="cite_ref-NGB_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NGB-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> His first professional short story publication was "Featherquest", a fantasy story, in <i><a href="/wiki/Imagine_(AD%26D_magazine)" class="mw-redirect" title="Imagine (AD&amp;D magazine)">Imagine Magazine</a></i> in May 1984.<sup id="cite_ref-NGB_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NGB-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>When waiting for a train at London\'s <a href="/wiki/London_Victoria_station" title="London Victoria station">Victoria Station</a> in 1984, Gaiman noticed a copy of <i><a href="/wiki/Swamp_Thing" title="Swamp Thing">Swamp Thing</a></i> written by <a href="/wiki/Alan_Moore" title="Alan Moore">Alan Moore</a>, and carefully read it. Moore\'s fresh and vigorous approach to comics had such an impact on Gaiman that he would later write "that was the final straw, what was left of my resistance crumbled. I proceeded to make regular and frequent visits to London\'s <a href="/wiki/Forbidden_Planet_(bookstore)" title="Forbidden Planet (bookstore)">Forbidden Planet</a> shop to buy comics".<sup id="cite_ref-MOR_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MOR-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1984, he wrote his first book, a biography of the band <a href="/wiki/Duran_Duran" title="Duran Duran">Duran Duran</a>, as well as <i>Ghastly Beyond Belief</i>, a book of quotations, with <a href="/wiki/Kim_Newman" title="Kim Newman">Kim Newman</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Abbey_18-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abbey-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> Even though Gaiman thought he had done a terrible job, the book\'s first edition sold out very quickly. When he went to relinquish his rights to the book, he discovered the publisher had gone bankrupt.<sup id="cite_ref-Abbey_18-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abbey-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> After this, he was offered a job by <i><a href="/wiki/Penthouse_(magazine)" title="Penthouse (magazine)">Penthouse</a></i>. He refused the offer.<sup id="cite_ref-Abbey_18-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abbey-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>He also wrote interviews and articles for many British magazines, including <i><a href="/wiki/Knave_(magazine)" title="Knave (magazine)">Knave</a>.</i> During this he sometimes wrote under pseudonyms, including Gerry Musgrave, Richard Grey, and "a couple of house names".<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Gaiman has said he ended his journalism career in 1987 because British newspapers regularly publish untruths as fact.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>\nIn the late 1980s, he wrote <i><a href="/wiki/Don%27t_Panic:_The_Official_Hitchhiker%27s_Guide_to_the_Galaxy_Companion" title="Don&#39;t Panic: The Official Hitchhiker&#39;s Guide to the Galaxy Companion">Don\'t Panic: The Official Hitchhiker\'s Guide to the Galaxy Companion</a></i> in what he calls a "classic English humour" style.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> Following this he wrote the opening of what would become his collaboration with fellow English author <a href="/wiki/Terry_Pratchett" title="Terry Pratchett">Terry Pratchett</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Comic_novel" title="Comic novel">comic novel</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Good_Omens" title="Good Omens">Good Omens</a></i>, about the impending apocalypse.<sup id="cite_ref-Omens_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Omens-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Comics">Comics</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Neil_Gaiman_bibliography#Comics" title="Neil Gaiman bibliography">Neil Gaiman bibliography \xc2\xa7&#160;Comics</a></div>\n<p>After forming a friendship with comic-book writer <a href="/wiki/Alan_Moore" title="Alan Moore">Alan Moore</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-MOR_25-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MOR-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> Gaiman started writing comic-books, picking up <i><a href="/wiki/Marvelman" title="Marvelman">Miracleman</a></i> after Moore finished his run on the series. Gaiman and artist <a href="/wiki/Mark_Buckingham" title="Mark Buckingham">Mark Buckingham</a> collaborated on several issues of the series before its publisher, <a href="/wiki/Eclipse_Comics" title="Eclipse Comics">Eclipse Comics</a>, collapsed, leaving the series unfinished. His first published comic strips were four short <i><a href="/wiki/Future_Shocks" class="mw-redirect" title="Future Shocks">Future Shocks</a></i> for <i><a href="/wiki/2000_AD_(comics)" title="2000 AD (comics)">2000 AD</a></i> in 1986\xe2\x80\x9387. He wrote three graphic novels with his favourite collaborator and long-time friend <a href="/wiki/Dave_McKean" title="Dave McKean">Dave McKean</a>: <i><a href="/wiki/Violent_Cases" title="Violent Cases">Violent Cases</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Signal_to_Noise_(comics)" title="Signal to Noise (comics)">Signal to Noise</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Tragical_Comedy_or_Comical_Tragedy_of_Mr._Punch" title="The Tragical Comedy or Comical Tragedy of Mr. Punch">The Tragical Comedy or Comical Tragedy of Mr. Punch</a></i>. Impressed with his work, <a href="/wiki/DC_Comics" title="DC Comics">DC Comics</a> hired him in February 1987,<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> and he wrote the limited series <i><a href="/wiki/Black_Orchid_(comic_book)" title="Black Orchid (comic book)">Black Orchid</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-vert-ency_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vert-ency-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Karen_Berger" title="Karen Berger">Karen Berger</a>, who later became head of <a href="/wiki/DC_Comics" title="DC Comics">DC Comics</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Vertigo_(DC_Comics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Vertigo (DC Comics)">Vertigo</a>, read <i>Black Orchid</i> and offered Gaiman a job: to re-write an old character, The Sandman, but to put his own spin on him.<sup id="cite_ref-Abbey_18-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abbey-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><i><a href="/wiki/The_Sandman_(Vertigo)" title="The Sandman (Vertigo)">The Sandman</a></i> tells the tale of the <a href="/wiki/Endless_(comics)" title="Endless (comics)">ageless</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anthropomorphic" class="mw-redirect" title="Anthropomorphic">anthropomorphic</a> personification of <a href="/wiki/Dream_(comics)" title="Dream (comics)">Dream</a> that is known by many names, including <a href="/wiki/Dream_(comics)" title="Dream (comics)">Morpheus</a>. The series began in January 1989 and concluded in March 1996.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> In the eighth issue of <i>The Sandman</i>, Gaiman and artist <a href="/wiki/Mike_Dringenberg" title="Mike Dringenberg">Mike Dringenberg</a> introduced <a href="/wiki/Death_(DC_Comics)" title="Death (DC Comics)">Death</a>, the older sister of Dream, who would become as popular as the series\' title character.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> The limited series <i><a href="/wiki/Death:_The_High_Cost_of_Living" title="Death: The High Cost of Living">Death: The High Cost of Living</a></i> launched DC\'s <a href="/wiki/Vertigo_(DC_Comics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Vertigo (DC Comics)">Vertigo</a> line in 1993.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> The 75 issues of the regular series, along with an illustrated prose text and a special containing seven short stories, have been collected into 12 volumes that remain in print, 14 if the <i>Death: The High Cost of Living</i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Death:_The_Time_of_Your_Life" title="Death: The Time of Your Life">Death: The Time of Your Life</a></i> spin-offs are included. Artists include <a href="/wiki/Sam_Kieth" title="Sam Kieth">Sam Kieth</a>, Mike Dringenberg, <a href="/wiki/Jill_Thompson" title="Jill Thompson">Jill Thompson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shawn_McManus" title="Shawn McManus">Shawn McManus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marc_Hempel" title="Marc Hempel">Marc Hempel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Michael_Zulli" title="Michael Zulli">Michael Zulli</a>, lettering by <a href="/wiki/Todd_Klein" title="Todd Klein">Todd Klein</a>, colours by Daniel Vozzo, and covers by <a href="/wiki/Dave_McKean" title="Dave McKean">Dave McKean</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Abbey_18-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abbey-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> The series became one of DC\'s top selling titles, eclipsing even <i>Batman</i> and <i>Superman</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> Comics historian <a href="/wiki/Les_Daniels" title="Les Daniels">Les Daniels</a> called Gaiman\'s work "astonishing" and noted that <i>The Sandman</i> was "a mixture of fantasy, horror, and ironic humor such as comic books had never seen before".<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> DC Comics writer and executive <a href="/wiki/Paul_Levitz" title="Paul Levitz">Paul Levitz</a> observed that "<i>The Sandman</i> became the first extraordinary success as a series of graphic novel collections, reaching out and converting new readers to the medium, particularly young women on college campuses, and making Gaiman himself into an iconic cultural figure."<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Gaiman and <a href="/wiki/Jamie_Delano" title="Jamie Delano">Jamie Delano</a> were to become co-writers of the <i><a href="/wiki/Swamp_Thing_(comic_book)" title="Swamp Thing (comic book)">Swamp Thing</a></i> series following <a href="/wiki/Rick_Veitch" title="Rick Veitch">Rick Veitch</a>. An editorial decision by DC to censor Veitch\'s final storyline caused both Gaiman and Delano to withdraw from the title.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Gaiman produced two stories for DC\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Secret_Origins" title="Secret Origins">Secret Origins</a></i> series in 1989. A <a href="/wiki/Poison_Ivy_(comics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Poison Ivy (comics)">Poison Ivy</a><sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> tale drawn by <a href="/wiki/Mark_Buckingham" title="Mark Buckingham">Mark Buckingham</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Riddler" title="Riddler">Riddler</a><sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> story illustrated by <a href="/wiki/Bernie_Mireault" title="Bernie Mireault">Bernie Mireault</a> and <a href="/wiki/Matt_Wagner" title="Matt Wagner">Matt Wagner</a>. A story which Gaiman originally wrote for <i><a href="/wiki/Action_Comics" title="Action Comics">Action Comics Weekly</a></i> in 1989 was shelved due to editorial concerns but it was finally published in 2000 as <i><a href="/wiki/Green_Lantern/Superman:_Legend_of_the_Green_Flame" title="Green Lantern/Superman: Legend of the Green Flame">Green Lantern/Superman: Legend of the Green Flame</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1990, Gaiman wrote <i><a href="/wiki/The_Books_of_Magic" title="The Books of Magic">The Books of Magic</a></i>, a four-part mini-series that provided a tour of the mythological and magical parts of the <a href="/wiki/DC_Universe" title="DC Universe">DC Universe</a> through a <a href="/wiki/Frame_story" title="Frame story">frame story</a> about an English teenager who discovers that he is destined to be the world\'s greatest wizard.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> The miniseries was popular, and sired an ongoing series written by <a href="/wiki/John_Ney_Rieber" title="John Ney Rieber">John Ney Rieber</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-sj_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sj-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Gaiman\'s adaptation of <i><a href="/wiki/Sweeney_Todd" title="Sweeney Todd">Sweeney Todd</a></i>, illustrated by <a href="/wiki/Michael_Zulli" title="Michael Zulli">Michael Zulli</a> for <a href="/wiki/Stephen_R._Bissette" title="Stephen R. Bissette">Stephen R. Bissette</a>\'s publication <i><a href="/wiki/Taboo_(comics)" title="Taboo (comics)">Taboo</a></i>, was stopped when the anthology itself was discontinued.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the mid-1990s, he also created a number of new characters and a setting that was to be featured in a title published by <a href="/wiki/Tekno_Comix" title="Tekno Comix">Tekno Comix</a>. The concepts were then altered and split between three titles set in the same continuity:  <i><a href="/wiki/Lady_Justice_(comics)" title="Lady Justice (comics)">Lady Justice</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Mr._Hero_the_Newmatic_Man" title="Mr. Hero the Newmatic Man">Mr. Hero the Newmatic Man</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Teknophage" class="mw-redirect" title="Teknophage">Teknophage</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> and tie-ins. Although Gaiman\'s name appeared prominently as creator of the characters, he was not involved in writing any of the above-mentioned books.\n</p><p>Gaiman wrote a semi-autobiographical story about a boy\'s fascination with <a href="/wiki/Michael_Moorcock" title="Michael Moorcock">Michael Moorcock</a>\'s anti-hero <a href="/wiki/Elric_of_Melnibon%C3%A9" title="Elric of Melnibon\xc3\xa9">Elric of Melnibon\xc3\xa9</a> for <a href="/wiki/Ed_Kramer" class="mw-redirect" title="Ed Kramer">Ed Kramer</a>\'s anthology <i>Tales of the White Wolf.</i> In 1996, Gaiman and <a href="/wiki/Ed_Kramer" class="mw-redirect" title="Ed Kramer">Ed Kramer</a> co-edited <i><a href="/wiki/The_Sandman:_Book_of_Dreams" title="The Sandman: Book of Dreams">The Sandman: Book of Dreams</a></i>. Nominated for the <a href="/wiki/British_Fantasy_Award" title="British Fantasy Award">British Fantasy Award</a>, the original fiction anthology featured stories and contributions by <a href="/wiki/Tori_Amos" title="Tori Amos">Tori Amos</a>, <a href="/wiki/Clive_Barker" title="Clive Barker">Clive Barker</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gene_Wolfe" title="Gene Wolfe">Gene Wolfe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tad_Williams" title="Tad Williams">Tad Williams</a>, and others.\n</p><p>Asked why he likes comics more than other forms of storytelling, Gaiman said: "One of the joys of comics has always been the knowledge that it was, in many ways, untouched ground. It was virgin territory. When I was working on <i>Sandman</i>, I felt a lot of the time that I was actually picking up a machete and heading out into the jungle. I got to write in places and do things that nobody had ever done before. When I\xe2\x80\x99m writing novels I\xe2\x80\x99m painfully aware that I\xe2\x80\x99m working in a medium that people have been writing absolutely jaw-droppingly brilliant things for, you know, three-four thousand years now. You know, you can go back. We have things like <i><a href="/wiki/The_Golden_Ass" title="The Golden Ass">The Golden Ass</a></i>. And you go, well, I don\xe2\x80\x99t know that I\xe2\x80\x99m as good as that and that\'s two and a half thousand years old. But with comics I felt like \xe2\x80\x93 I can do stuff nobody has ever done. I can do stuff nobody has ever thought of. And I could and it was enormously fun."<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Gaiman wrote two series for <a href="/wiki/Marvel_Comics" title="Marvel Comics">Marvel Comics</a>. <i><a href="/wiki/Marvel_1602" title="Marvel 1602">Marvel 1602</a></i> was an eight-issue limited series published from November 2003 to June 2004 with art by <a href="/wiki/Andy_Kubert" title="Andy Kubert">Andy Kubert</a> and <a href="/wiki/Richard_Isanove" title="Richard Isanove">Richard Isanove</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> <i>The <a href="/wiki/Eternals_(comics)" title="Eternals (comics)">Eternals</a></i> was a seven-issue limited series drawn by <a href="/wiki/John_Romita_Jr." title="John Romita Jr.">John Romita Jr.</a> which was published from August 2006 to March 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2009, Gaiman wrote a two-part <a href="/wiki/Batman" title="Batman">Batman</a> story for DC Comics to follow <i><a href="/wiki/Batman_R.I.P." title="Batman R.I.P.">Batman R.I.P.</a></i> titled "<a href="/wiki/Batman:_Whatever_Happened_to_the_Caped_Crusader%3F" title="Batman: Whatever Happened to the Caped Crusader?">Whatever Happened to the Caped Crusader?</a>"<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> a play-off of the classic Superman story "<a href="/wiki/Superman:_Whatever_Happened_to_the_Man_of_Tomorrow%3F" title="Superman: Whatever Happened to the Man of Tomorrow?">Whatever Happened to the Man of Tomorrow?</a>" by Alan Moore.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> He contributed a twelve-part <a href="/wiki/Metamorpho" title="Metamorpho">Metamorpho</a> serial drawn by <a href="/wiki/Mike_Allred" title="Mike Allred">Mike Allred</a> for <i><a href="/wiki/Wednesday_Comics" title="Wednesday Comics">Wednesday Comics</a></i>, a weekly newspaper-style series.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> Gaiman and <a href="/wiki/Paul_Cornell" title="Paul Cornell">Paul Cornell</a> co-wrote <i><a href="/wiki/Action_Comics" title="Action Comics">Action Comics</a></i> #894 (Dec. 2010) which featured an appearance by Death.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> In October 2013, DC Comics released <i><a href="/wiki/The_Sandman:_Overture" title="The Sandman: Overture">The Sandman: Overture</a></i> with art by <a href="/wiki/J._H._Williams_III" title="J. H. Williams III">J. H. Williams III</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> Gaiman\'s <a href="/wiki/Angela_(comics)" title="Angela (comics)">Angela</a> character was introduced into the Marvel Universe in the last issue of the <i><a href="/wiki/Age_of_Ultron" title="Age of Ultron">Age of Ultron</a></i> miniseries in 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-Sunu_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sunu-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Gaiman is overseeing <i><a href="/wiki/The_Sandman_Universe" title="The Sandman Universe">The Sandman Universe</a></i>, a line of comic books published by Vertigo. The four new ongoing series \xe2\x80\x94<i>House of Whispers</i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Lucifer_(DC_Comics)" title="Lucifer (DC Comics)">Lucifer</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Books_of_Magic" title="The Books of Magic">The Books of Magic</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Dreaming_(comics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Dreaming (comics)">The Dreaming</a></i> are written by new creative teams. The line launched on 8 August 2018.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Novels">Novels</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Gaiman-headshot.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Gaiman-headshot.jpg/220px-Gaiman-headshot.jpg" width="220" height="235" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Gaiman-headshot.jpg/330px-Gaiman-headshot.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Gaiman-headshot.jpg/440px-Gaiman-headshot.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1495" data-file-height="1600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Gaiman-headshot.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Gaiman in 2009</div></div></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Neil_Gaiman_bibliography#Novels_and_children&#39;s_books" title="Neil Gaiman bibliography">Neil Gaiman bibliography \xc2\xa7&#160;Novels and children\'s books</a></div>\n<p>In a collaboration with author <a href="/wiki/Terry_Pratchett" title="Terry Pratchett">Terry Pratchett</a>, best known for his series of <i><a href="/wiki/Discworld" title="Discworld">Discworld</a></i> novels, Gaiman\'s first novel <i><a href="/wiki/Good_Omens" title="Good Omens">Good Omens</a></i> was published in 1990. In 2011 Pratchett said that while the entire novel was a collaborative effort and most of the ideas could be credited to both of them, Pratchett did a larger portion of writing and editing if for no other reason than Gaiman\'s scheduled involvement with <i>Sandman</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The 1996 <a href="/wiki/Neverwhere_(novel)" title="Neverwhere (novel)">novelisation</a> of Gaiman\'s teleplay for the BBC mini-series <i><a href="/wiki/Neverwhere" title="Neverwhere">Neverwhere</a></i> was his first solo novel. The novel was released in tandem with the television series though it presents some notable differences from the television series. Gaiman has since revised the novel twice, the first time for an American audience unfamiliar with the <a href="/wiki/London_Underground" title="London Underground">London Underground</a>, the second time because he felt unsatisfied with the original.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>In 1999, first printings of his fantasy novel <i><a href="/wiki/Stardust_(novel)" title="Stardust (novel)">Stardust</a></i> were released. The novel has been released both as a standard novel and in an illustrated text edition.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> This novel was highly influenced by Victorian fairytales and culture.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><i><a href="/wiki/American_Gods" title="American Gods">American Gods</a></i> became one of Gaiman\'s best-selling and multi-award-winning novels upon its release in 2001.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> A special 10th Anniversary edition was released, with the "author\'s preferred text" 12,000 words longer than the original mass-market editions.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>Gaiman has not written a direct sequel to <i>American Gods</i> but he has revisited the characters. A glimpse at Shadow\'s travels in Europe is found in a short story which finds him in Scotland, applying the same concepts developed in <i>American Gods</i> to the story of <i><a href="/wiki/Beowulf" title="Beowulf">Beowulf</a></i>. The 2005 novel <i><a href="/wiki/Anansi_Boys" title="Anansi Boys">Anansi Boys</a></i>  deals with <a href="/wiki/Anansi" title="Anansi">Anansi</a> (\'Mr. Nancy\'), tracing the relationship of his two sons, one semi-divine and the other an unassuming Englishman, as they explore their common heritage. It debuted at number one on <a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times_Best_Seller_list" title="The New York Times Best Seller list"><i>The New York Times</i> Best Seller list</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-NYT_2005-10-09_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT_2005-10-09-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In late 2008, Gaiman released a new children\'s book, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Graveyard_Book" title="The Graveyard Book">The Graveyard Book</a></i>. It follows the adventures of a boy named Bod after his family is murdered and he is left to be brought up by a graveyard. It is heavily influenced by <a href="/wiki/Rudyard_Kipling" title="Rudyard Kipling">Rudyard Kipling</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Jungle_Book" title="The Jungle Book">The Jungle Book</a></i>. As of late January 2009<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Neil_Gaiman&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, it had been on <i>The New York Times</i> Bestseller children\'s list for fifteen weeks.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2013, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Ocean_at_the_End_of_the_Lane" title="The Ocean at the End of the Lane">The Ocean at the End of the Lane</a></i> was voted Book of the Year in the British <a href="/wiki/Specsavers_National_Book_Awards" class="mw-redirect" title="Specsavers National Book Awards">National Book Awards</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-BOTY_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BOTY-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> The novel follows an unnamed man who returns to his hometown for a funeral and remembers events that began forty years earlier.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>  Themes include the search for <a href="/wiki/Self-identity" class="mw-redirect" title="Self-identity">self-identity</a> and the "disconnect between childhood and adulthood".<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In September 2016, Neil Gaiman announced that he had been working for some years on a retellings of <a href="/wiki/Norse_mythology" title="Norse mythology">Norse mythology</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Norse_Mythology_(Neil_Gaiman)" title="Norse Mythology (Neil Gaiman)">Norse Mythology</a></i> was released in February 2017.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Film_and_screenwriting">Film and screenwriting</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Neil_Gaiman_bibliography#Film" title="Neil Gaiman bibliography">Neil Gaiman bibliography \xc2\xa7&#160;Film</a></div>\n<p>Gaiman wrote the 1996 BBC dark fantasy television series <i><a href="/wiki/Neverwhere" title="Neverwhere">Neverwhere</a></i>. He cowrote the screenplay for the movie <i><a href="/wiki/MirrorMask" title="MirrorMask">MirrorMask</a></i> with his old friend <a href="/wiki/Dave_McKean" title="Dave McKean">Dave McKean</a> for McKean to direct. In addition, he wrote the localised English language script to the <a href="/wiki/Anime" title="Anime">anime</a> movie <i><a href="/wiki/Princess_Mononoke" title="Princess Mononoke">Princess Mononoke</a></i>, based on a translation of the Japanese script.<sup id="cite_ref-princescripts_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-princescripts-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>He cowrote the script for <a href="/wiki/Robert_Zemeckis" title="Robert Zemeckis">Robert Zemeckis</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Beowulf_(2007_film)" title="Beowulf (2007 film)">Beowulf</a></i> with <a href="/wiki/Roger_Avary" title="Roger Avary">Roger Avary</a>, a collaboration that has proved productive for both writers.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> Gaiman has expressed interest in collaborating on a <a href="/wiki/Film_adaptation" title="Film adaptation">film adaptation</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Epic_of_Gilgamesh" title="Epic of Gilgamesh">Epic of Gilgamesh</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>He was the only person other than <a href="/wiki/J._Michael_Straczynski" title="J. Michael Straczynski">J. Michael Straczynski</a> to write a <i><a href="/wiki/Babylon_5" title="Babylon 5">Babylon 5</a></i> script in the last three seasons, contributing the season five episode "<a href="/wiki/Day_of_the_Dead_(Babylon_5)" title="Day of the Dead (Babylon 5)">Day of the Dead</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-princescripts_79-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-princescripts-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Gaiman has also written at least three drafts of a screenplay adaptation of <a href="/wiki/Nicholson_Baker" title="Nicholson Baker">Nicholson Baker</a>\'s novel <i><a href="/wiki/The_Fermata" title="The Fermata">The Fermata</a></i> for director <a href="/wiki/Robert_Zemeckis" title="Robert Zemeckis">Robert Zemeckis</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> although the project was stalled while Zemeckis made <i><a href="/wiki/The_Polar_Express_(film)" title="The Polar Express (film)">The Polar Express</a></i> and the Gaiman-<a href="/wiki/Roger_Avary" title="Roger Avary">Roger Avary</a> written <i><a href="/wiki/Beowulf_(2007_film)" title="Beowulf (2007 film)">Beowulf</a></i> film.\n</p><p>Neil Gaiman was featured in the <i><a href="/wiki/History_(U.S._TV_channel)" class="mw-redirect" title="History (U.S. TV channel)">History Channel</a></i> documentary <i><a href="/wiki/Comic_Book_Superheroes_Unmasked" title="Comic Book Superheroes Unmasked">Comic Book Superheroes Unmasked</a></i>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>Several of Gaiman\'s original works have been optioned or greenlighted for film adaptation, most notably <i><a href="/wiki/Stardust_(2007_film)" title="Stardust (2007 film)">Stardust</a></i>, which premiered in August 2007 and stars <a href="/wiki/Charlie_Cox" title="Charlie Cox">Charlie Cox</a>, <a href="/wiki/Robert_De_Niro" title="Robert De Niro">Robert De Niro</a>, <a href="/wiki/Michelle_Pfeiffer" title="Michelle Pfeiffer">Michelle Pfeiffer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Claire_Danes" title="Claire Danes">Claire Danes</a>, directed by <a href="/wiki/Matthew_Vaughn" title="Matthew Vaughn">Matthew Vaughn</a>. A stop-motion version of <i><a href="/wiki/Coraline_(film)" title="Coraline (film)">Coraline</a></i> was released on 6 February 2009, with <a href="/wiki/Henry_Selick" title="Henry Selick">Henry Selick</a> directing and <a href="/wiki/Dakota_Fanning" title="Dakota Fanning">Dakota Fanning</a> and <a href="/wiki/Teri_Hatcher" title="Teri Hatcher">Teri Hatcher</a> in the leading voice-actor roles.<sup id="cite_ref-Goodyear_14-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Goodyear-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2007, Gaiman it was announced that after ten years in development, <a href="/wiki/Death_and_Me" class="mw-redirect" title="Death and Me">the feature film</a> of <i><a href="/wiki/Death:_The_High_Cost_of_Living" title="Death: The High Cost of Living">Death: The High Cost of Living</a></i> would finally begin production with a screenplay by Gaiman that he would direct for Warner Independent. <a href="/wiki/Don_Murphy" title="Don Murphy">Don Murphy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Susan_Montford" title="Susan Montford">Susan Montford</a> are the producers, and <a href="/wiki/Guillermo_del_Toro" title="Guillermo del Toro">Guillermo del Toro</a> is the film\'s executive producer.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> By 2010 it had been reported that it was no longer in production.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Seeing Ear Theatre performed two of Gaiman\'s audio theatre plays, "<a href="/wiki/Snow,_Glass,_Apples" title="Snow, Glass, Apples">Snow, Glass, Apples</a>", Gaiman\'s retelling of <a href="/wiki/Snow_White" title="Snow White">Snow White</a> and "<a href="/wiki/Murder_Mysteries" title="Murder Mysteries">Murder Mysteries</a>", a story of heaven before the Fall in which the first crime is committed. Both audio plays were published in the collection <i><a href="/wiki/Smoke_and_Mirrors_(story_collection)" class="mw-redirect" title="Smoke and Mirrors (story collection)">Smoke and Mirrors</a></i> in 1998.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Gaiman\'s 2009 <a href="/wiki/Newbery_Medal" title="Newbery Medal">Newbery Medal</a> winning book <i><a href="/wiki/The_Graveyard_Book" title="The Graveyard Book">The Graveyard Book</a></i> will be made into a movie, with <a href="/wiki/Ron_Howard" title="Ron Howard">Ron Howard</a> as the director.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Gaiman wrote an episode of the long-running BBC science fiction series <i><a href="/wiki/Doctor_Who" title="Doctor Who">Doctor Who</a></i>, broadcast in 2011 during <a href="/wiki/Matt_Smith_(actor)" title="Matt Smith (actor)">Matt Smith</a>\'s second series as the Doctor.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> Shooting began in August 2010 for this episode, the original title of which was "The House of Nothing"<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> but which was eventually transmitted as "<a href="/wiki/The_Doctor%27s_Wife" title="The Doctor&#39;s Wife">The Doctor\'s Wife</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> The episode won the 2012 <a href="/wiki/Hugo_Award_for_Best_Dramatic_Presentation,_Short_Form" class="mw-redirect" title="Hugo Award for Best Dramatic Presentation, Short Form">Hugo Award for Best Dramatic Presentation (Short Form)</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Davis_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Davis-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated3_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated3-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> Gaiman made his return to <i>Doctor Who</i> with an episode titled "<a href="/wiki/Nightmare_in_Silver" title="Nightmare in Silver">Nightmare in Silver</a>", broadcast on 11 May 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2011, it was announced that Gaiman would be writing the script to a new film version of <i><a href="/wiki/Journey_to_the_West" title="Journey to the West">Journey to the West</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Gaiman appeared as himself on <i><a href="/wiki/The_Simpsons" title="The Simpsons">The Simpsons</a></i> episode "<a href="/wiki/The_Book_Job" title="The Book Job">The Book Job</a>" broadcast on 20 November 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2015, <a href="/wiki/Starz" title="Starz">Starz</a> greenlighted a <a href="/wiki/American_Gods_(TV_series)" title="American Gods (TV series)">series adaptation</a> of Gaiman\'s novel <i><a href="/wiki/American_Gods" title="American Gods">American Gods</a></i>. <a href="/wiki/Bryan_Fuller" title="Bryan Fuller">Bryan Fuller</a> and <a href="/wiki/Michael_Green_(writer)" title="Michael Green (writer)">Michael Green</a> will write and showrun the series.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Radio">Radio</span></h3>\n<p>A six-part <a href="/wiki/Neverwhere_(radio_play)" title="Neverwhere (radio play)">radio play of <i>Neverwhere</i></a> was broadcast in March 2013, adapted by Dirk Maggs for <a href="/wiki/BBC_Radio_4" title="BBC Radio 4">BBC Radio 4</a> and Radio 4 Extra. Featured stars include <a href="/wiki/James_McAvoy" title="James McAvoy">James McAvoy</a> as Richard, <a href="/wiki/Natalie_Dormer" title="Natalie Dormer">Natalie Dormer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Benedict_Cumberbatch" title="Benedict Cumberbatch">Benedict Cumberbatch</a>, <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Lee" title="Christopher Lee">Christopher Lee</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bernard_Cribbens" class="mw-redirect" title="Bernard Cribbens">Bernard Cribbens</a> and <a href="/wiki/Johnny_Vegas" title="Johnny Vegas">Johnny Vegas</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC_Radio_Neverwhere_2013_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_Radio_Neverwhere_2013-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In September 2014, Gaiman and Terry Pratchett joined forces with BBC Radio 4 to make the first ever dramatisation of their co-penned novel <i>Good Omens</i>, which was broadcast in December in five half-hour episodes and culminated in an hour-long final apocalyptic showdown.<sup id="cite_ref-Omens_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Omens-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Public_performances">Public performances</span></h3>\n<p>Gaiman frequently performs public readings from his stories and poetry, and has toured with his wife, musician <a href="/wiki/Amanda_Palmer" title="Amanda Palmer">Amanda Palmer</a>. In some of these performances he has also sung songs, in "a novelist\'s version of singing",<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> despite having "no kind of singing voice".<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2015, Gaiman delivered a 100-minute lecture for the <a href="/wiki/Long_Now_Foundation" title="Long Now Foundation">Long Now Foundation</a> entitled <i>How Stories Last</i> about the nature of storytelling and how stories persist in human culture.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> In April 2018 Gaiman made a guest appearance on the television show <i><a href="/wiki/The_Big_Bang_Theory" title="The Big Bang Theory">The Big Bang Theory</a></i>, and his tweet about the show\'s fictional comic book store becomes the central theme of the episode "The Comet Polarization".<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Blog_and_Twitter">Blog and Twitter</span></h3>\n<p>In February 2001, when Gaiman had completed writing <i><a href="/wiki/American_Gods" title="American Gods">American Gods</a></i>, his publishers set up a promotional website featuring a <a href="/wiki/Weblog" class="mw-redirect" title="Weblog">weblog</a> in which Gaiman described the day-to-day process of revising, publishing, and promoting the novel. After the novel was published, the website evolved into a more general Official Neil Gaiman Website.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Gaiman generally posts to the blog describing the day-to-day process of being Neil Gaiman and writing, revising, publishing, or promoting whatever the current project is. He also posts reader emails and answers questions, which gives him unusually direct and immediate interaction with fans. One of his answers on why he writes the blog is "because writing is, like death, a lonely business."<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The original <i>American Gods</i> blog was extracted for publication in the <a href="/wiki/NESFA_Press" title="NESFA Press">NESFA Press</a> collection of Gaiman miscellany, <i>Adventures in the Dream Trade</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>To celebrate the seventh anniversary of the blog, the novel <i>American Gods</i> was provided free of charge online for a month.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Gaiman is an active user of the social networking site Twitter with over 2.7 million followers as of  June&#160;2018<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Neil_Gaiman&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, using the username <i>@neilhimself</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> In 2013, Gaiman was named by <a href="/wiki/IGN" title="IGN">IGN</a> as one of "The Best Tweeters in Comics", describing his posts as "sublime."<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> Gaiman also runs a <a href="/wiki/Tumblr" title="Tumblr">Tumblr</a> account on which he primarily answers fan questions.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span
