id="Personal_life">Personal life</span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Home_and_family">Home and family</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Amanda_Palmer_-_Arena_Vienna_2011_a10_Neil_Gaiman.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Amanda_Palmer_-_Arena_Vienna_2011_a10_Neil_Gaiman.jpg/220px-Amanda_Palmer_-_Arena_Vienna_2011_a10_Neil_Gaiman.jpg" width="220" height="138" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Amanda_Palmer_-_Arena_Vienna_2011_a10_Neil_Gaiman.jpg/330px-Amanda_Palmer_-_Arena_Vienna_2011_a10_Neil_Gaiman.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Amanda_Palmer_-_Arena_Vienna_2011_a10_Neil_Gaiman.jpg/440px-Amanda_Palmer_-_Arena_Vienna_2011_a10_Neil_Gaiman.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1920" data-file-height="1200" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Amanda_Palmer_-_Arena_Vienna_2011_a10_Neil_Gaiman.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Neil Gaiman and <a href="/wiki/Amanda_Palmer" title="Amanda Palmer">Amanda Palmer</a> (Vienna 2011)</div></div></div>\n<p>Gaiman lives near <a href="/wiki/Menomonie,_Wisconsin" title="Menomonie, Wisconsin">Menomonie, Wisconsin</a>, United States and has lived there since 1992. Gaiman moved there to be close to the family of his then-wife, Mary McGrath, with whom he has three children: Michael, Holly, and Madeleine.<sup id="cite_ref-Abbey_18-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abbey-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-tg121205_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tg121205-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup>  As of  2013<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Neil_Gaiman&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, Gaiman also resides in <a href="/wiki/Cambridge,_Massachusetts" title="Cambridge, Massachusetts">Cambridge, Massachusetts</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> In 2014, he took up a five-year appointment as professor in the arts at <a href="/wiki/Bard_College" title="Bard College">Bard College</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Annandale-on-Hudson,_New_York" title="Annandale-on-Hudson, New York">Annandale-on-Hudson, New York</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Gaiman is married to songwriter and performer <a href="/wiki/Amanda_Palmer" title="Amanda Palmer">Amanda Palmer</a>, with whom he has an <a href="/wiki/Open_marriage" title="Open marriage">open marriage</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> The couple publicly announced that they were dating in June 2009,<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-GaimanPalmer_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GaimanPalmer-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> and announced their engagement on Twitter on 1 January 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> On 16 November 2010, Amanda Palmer hosted a non-legally binding <a href="/wiki/Flash_mob" title="Flash mob">flash mob</a> wedding for Gaiman\'s birthday in <a href="/wiki/New_Orleans" title="New Orleans">New Orleans</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> They were legally married on 2 January 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-Ology_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ology-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> The wedding took place in the parlour of writers <a href="/wiki/Ayelet_Waldman" title="Ayelet Waldman">Ayelet Waldman</a> and <a href="/wiki/Michael_Chabon" title="Michael Chabon">Michael Chabon</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-LexMinMan20110114_4-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LexMinMan20110114-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> On marrying Palmer, he took her middle name, MacKinnon, as one of his names.<sup id="cite_ref-LexMinMan20110114_4-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LexMinMan20110114-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>  On 18 March 2015, they announced through their Facebook and Twitter accounts that Palmer was pregnant with their first child.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> Their son Anthony was born 16 September 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Advocacy">Advocacy</span></h3>\n<p>In 2016, Gaiman, as well as <a href="/wiki/Cate_Blanchett" title="Cate Blanchett">Cate Blanchett</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chiwetel_Ejiofor" title="Chiwetel Ejiofor">Chiwetel Ejiofor</a>, <a href="/wiki/Peter_Capaldi" title="Peter Capaldi">Peter Capaldi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Douglas_Booth" title="Douglas Booth">Douglas Booth</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jesse_Eisenberg" title="Jesse Eisenberg">Jesse Eisenberg</a>, <a href="/wiki/Keira_Knightley" title="Keira Knightley">Keira Knightley</a>, <a href="/wiki/Juliet_Stevenson" title="Juliet Stevenson">Juliet Stevenson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kit_Harington" title="Kit Harington">Kit Harington</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Stanley_Tucci" title="Stanley Tucci">Stanley Tucci</a>, appear in the video "What They Took With Them", from the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_System" title="United Nations System">United Nations\'</a> refugee agency <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_High_Commissioner_for_Refugees" title="United Nations High Commissioner for Refugees">UNHCR</a>, to help raise awareness of the issue of global refugees.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Gaiman is a supporter and board member of the <a href="/wiki/Comic_Book_Legal_Defense_Fund" title="Comic Book Legal Defense Fund">Comic Book Legal Defense Fund</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Friendship_with_Tori_Amos">Friendship with Tori Amos</span></h3>\n<p>One of Gaiman\'s most commented-upon friendships is with the musician <a href="/wiki/Tori_Amos" title="Tori Amos">Tori Amos</a>, a <i>Sandman</i> fan who became friends with Gaiman after making a reference to "Neil and the Dream King" on her 1991 demo tape. He included her in turn as a character (a talking tree) in his novel <i>Stardust</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> Amos also mentions Gaiman in her songs, "Tear in Your Hand" ("If you need me, me and Neil\'ll be hangin\' out with the dream king. Neil says hi by the way"),<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> "Space Dog" ("Where\'s Neil when you need him?"),<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> "Horses" ("But will you find me if Neil makes me a tree?"),<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> "Carbon" ("Get me Neil on the line, no I can\'t hold. Have him read, \'Snow, Glass, Apples\' where nothing is what it seems"),<sup id="cite_ref-everythingtori.com_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-everythingtori.com-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> "Sweet Dreams" ("You\'re forgetting to fly, darling, when you sleep"),<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> and "Not Dying Today" ("Neil is thrilled he can claim he\'s mammalian, \'but the bad news,\' he said, \'girl you\'re a dandelion\'").<sup id="cite_ref-everythingtori.com_137-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-everythingtori.com-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> He also wrote stories for the tour book of <i><a href="/wiki/Boys_for_Pele" title="Boys for Pele">Boys for Pele</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Scarlet%27s_Walk" title="Scarlet&#39;s Walk">Scarlet\'s Walk</a></i>, a letter for the tour book of <i><a href="/wiki/American_Doll_Posse" title="American Doll Posse">American Doll Posse</a></i>, and the stories behind each girl in her album <i><a href="/wiki/Strange_Little_Girls" title="Strange Little Girls">Strange Little Girls</a></i>. Amos penned the introduction for his novel <i>Death: the High Cost of Living</i>, and posed for the cover. She also wrote a song called "Sister Named Desire" based on his <i>Sandman</i> character, which was included on his anthology, <i>Where\'s Neil When You Need Him?</i>.\n</p><p>Gaiman is godfather to Tori Amos\'s daughter Tash,<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> and wrote a poem called "Blueberry Girl" for Tori and Tash.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> The poem has been turned into a book by the illustrator <a href="/wiki/Charles_Vess" title="Charles Vess">Charles Vess</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> Gaiman read the poem aloud to an audience at the Sundance Kabuki Theater in San Francisco on 5 October 2008 during his book reading tour for <i>The Graveyard Book</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> It was published in March 2009 with the title <i><a href="/wiki/Blueberry_Girl" title="Blueberry Girl">Blueberry Girl</a></i>.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Litigation">Litigation</span></h2>\n<p>In 1993, Gaiman was contracted by <a href="/wiki/Todd_McFarlane" title="Todd McFarlane">Todd McFarlane</a> to write a single issue of <i><a href="/wiki/Spawn_(comics)" title="Spawn (comics)">Spawn</a></i>, a popular title at the newly created <a href="/wiki/Image_Comics" title="Image Comics">Image Comics</a> company. McFarlane was promoting his new title by having guest authors Gaiman, <a href="/wiki/Alan_Moore" title="Alan Moore">Alan Moore</a>, <a href="/wiki/Frank_Miller_(comics)" title="Frank Miller (comics)">Frank Miller</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Dave_Sim" title="Dave Sim">Dave Sim</a> each write a single issue.\n</p><p>In issue No. 9 of the series, Gaiman introduced the characters <a href="/wiki/Angela_(comics)" title="Angela (comics)">Angela</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cogliostro" title="Cogliostro">Cogliostro</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hellspawn_(Spawn)" title="Hellspawn (Spawn)">Medieval Spawn</a>. Prior to this issue, Spawn was an assassin who worked for the government and came back as a reluctant agent of Hell but had no direction. In Angela, a cruel and malicious angel, Gaiman introduced a character who threatened Spawn\'s existence, as well as providing a moral opposite. Cogliostro was introduced as a mentor character for exposition and instruction, providing guidance. Medieval Spawn introduced a history and precedent that not all Spawns were self-serving or evil, giving additional character development to <a href="/wiki/Malebolgia" title="Malebolgia">Malebolgia</a>, the demon that creates Hellspawn.\n</p><p>As intended,<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> all three characters were used repeatedly throughout the next decade by Todd McFarlane within the wider Spawn universe. In papers filed by  Gaiman in early 2002, however, he claimed that the characters were jointly owned by their scripter (himself) and artist (McFarlane), not merely by McFarlane in his role as the creator of the series.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> Disagreement over who owned the rights to a character was the primary motivation for McFarlane and other artists to form Image Comics (although that argument related more towards disagreements between writers and artists as character creators).<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> As McFarlane used the characters without Gaiman\'s permission or royalty payments, Gaiman believed his <a href="/wiki/Copyright" title="Copyright">copyrighted</a> work was being infringed upon, which violated their original, oral, agreement. McFarlane initially agreed that Gaiman had not signed away any rights to the characters, and negotiated with Gaiman to effectively \'swap\' McFarlane\'s interest in the character <a href="/wiki/Marvelman" title="Marvelman">Marvelman</a><sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup> (McFarlane believes he purchased interest in the character when <a href="/wiki/Eclipse_Comics" title="Eclipse Comics">Eclipse Comics</a> was liquidated; Gaiman is interested in being able to continue his aborted run of that title) but later claimed that Gaiman\'s work had been work-for-hire and that McFarlane owned all of Gaiman\'s creations entirely. The presiding judge, however, ruled against their agreement being work for hire, based in large part on the legal requirement that "copyright assignments must be in writing."<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Court_of_Appeals_for_the_Seventh_Circuit" title="United States Court of Appeals for the Seventh Circuit">Seventh Circuit Court of Appeals</a> upheld the district court ruling in February 2004<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> granting joint ownership of the characters to Gaiman and McFarlane. On the specific issue of Cogliostro, presiding Judge <a href="/wiki/John_C._Shabaz" title="John C. Shabaz">John C. Shabaz</a> proclaimed, "The expressive work that is the comic-book character Count Nicholas Cogliostro was the joint work of Gaiman and McFarlane\xe2\x80\x94their contributions strike us as quite equal\xe2\x80\x94and both are entitled to ownership of the copyright".<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup> Similar analysis led to similar results for the other two characters, Angela and Medieval Spawn.\n</p><p>This legal battle was brought by Gaiman and the specifically formed Marvels and Miracles, <a href="/wiki/Limited-liability_company" class="mw-redirect" title="Limited-liability company">LLC</a>, which Gaiman created to help sort out <a href="/wiki/Marvelman#Ownership_battle" title="Marvelman">the legal rights surrounding Marvelman</a>. Gaiman wrote <i><a href="/wiki/Marvel_1602" title="Marvel 1602">Marvel 1602</a> </i>in 2003 to help fund this project.<sup id="cite_ref-CBR1602_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CBR1602-151">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup> All of Marvel Comics\' profits for the original issues of the series went to Marvels and Miracles.<sup id="cite_ref-CBR1602_151-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CBR1602-151">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup> In 2009, Marvel Comics purchased Marvelman.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Gaiman returned to court over three more Spawn characters, <a href="/wiki/Dark_Ages_Spawn" class="mw-redirect" title="Dark Ages Spawn">Dark Ages Spawn</a>, <a href="/wiki/Domina_(Image_Comics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Domina (Image Comics)">Domina</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tiffany_(comics)" title="Tiffany (comics)">Tiffany</a>, that are claimed to be "derivative of the three he co-created with McFarlane."<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup> The judge ruled that Gaiman was right in his claims and gave McFarlane until the start of September 2010 to settle matters.<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Literary_allusions">Literary allusions</span></h2>\n<p>Gaiman\'s work is known for a high degree of <a href="/wiki/Allusion" title="Allusion">allusiveness</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup> Meredith Collins, for instance, has commented upon the degree to which his novel <i><a href="/wiki/Stardust_(novel)" title="Stardust (novel)">Stardust</a></i> depends on allusions to Victorian fairy tales and culture.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup> Particularly in <i>The Sandman</i>, literary figures and characters appear often; the character of Fiddler\'s Green is modelled visually on <a href="/wiki/G._K._Chesterton" title="G. K. Chesterton">G. K. Chesterton</a>, both <a href="/wiki/William_Shakespeare" title="William Shakespeare">William Shakespeare</a> and <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_Chaucer" title="Geoffrey Chaucer">Geoffrey Chaucer</a> appear as characters, as do several characters from within <i><a href="/wiki/A_Midsummer_Night%27s_Dream" title="A Midsummer Night&#39;s Dream">A Midsummer Night\'s Dream</a></i><sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Tempest" title="The Tempest">The Tempest</a></i>. The comic also draws from numerous mythologies and historical periods.\n</p><p>Analyzing Gaiman\'s <i>The Graveyard Book</i>, bibliographer and librarian <a href="/wiki/Richard_Bleiler" title="Richard Bleiler">Richard Bleiler</a> detects patterns of and allusions to the Gothic novel, from Horace Walpole\'s <a href="/wiki/The_Castle_of_Otranto" title="The Castle of Otranto">The Castle of Otranto</a> to Shirley Jackson\'s <a href="/wiki/The_Haunting_of_Hill_House" title="The Haunting of Hill House">The Haunting of Hill House</a>. He concludes that Gaiman is "utilizing works, characters, themes, and settings that generations of scholars have identified and classified as Gothic, ... [yet] subverts them and develops the novel by focusing on the positive aspects of maturation, concentrating on the values of learning, friendship, and sacrifice."<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> Regarding another work\'s assumed connection and allusions to this form, Gaiman himself quipped: "I\'ve never been able to figure out whether <i>Sandman</i> is a gothic."<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Clay Smith has argued that this sort of allusiveness serves to situate Gaiman as a strong authorial presence in his own works, often to the exclusion of his collaborators.<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup> However, Smith\'s viewpoint is in the minority: to many, if there is a problem with Gaiman scholarship and intertextuality it is that "... his literary merit and vast popularity have propelled him into the nascent comics canon so quickly that there is not yet a basis of critical scholarship about his work."<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>David Rudd takes a more generous view in his study of the novel <i><a href="/wiki/Coraline" title="Coraline">Coraline</a></i>, where he argues that the work plays and riffs productively on <a href="/wiki/Sigmund_Freud" title="Sigmund Freud">Sigmund Freud</a>\'s notion of <a href="/wiki/Uncanny" title="Uncanny">the Uncanny</a>, or the <i><a href="/wiki/Unheimlich" class="mw-redirect" title="Unheimlich">Unheimlich</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Though Gaiman\'s work is frequently seen as exemplifying the <a href="/wiki/Monomyth" class="mw-redirect" title="Monomyth">monomyth</a> structure laid out in <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Campbell" title="Joseph Campbell">Joseph Campbell</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Hero_with_a_Thousand_Faces" title="The Hero with a Thousand Faces">The Hero with a Thousand Faces</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup> Gaiman says that he started reading <i>The Hero with a Thousand Faces</i> but refused to finish it: "I think I got about half way through <i>The Hero with a Thousand Faces</i> and found myself thinking if this is true \xe2\x80\x93 I don\xe2\x80\x99t want to know. I really would rather not know this stuff. I\xe2\x80\x99d rather do it because it\xe2\x80\x99s true and because I accidentally wind up creating something that falls into this pattern than be told what the pattern is."<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Selected_awards_and_honours">Selected awards and honours</span></h2>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r853264625">.mw-parser-output .refbegin{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em;">\n<ul><li>From 1991 to 1993, Gaiman won <a href="/wiki/Harvey_Awards" class="mw-redirect" title="Harvey Awards">Harvey Awards</a> in the following categories:\n<ul><li>1991 Best Writer for <i>The Sandman</i><sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>1992 Best Awards">Locus Awards</a> in the following categories:\n<ul><li>1991 Best Fantasy Novel (runner-up) for <i><a href="/wiki/Good_Omens" title="Good Omens">Good Omens</a></i> by Gaiman and <a <i>Coraline</i><sup id="cite_ref-locus_168-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-locus-168">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a Boys</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-locus_168-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-locus-168">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup> The book was also nominated for a Hugo Award, but Gaiman asked for it to be withdrawn from the list, stating that he wanted to give other writers a chance and that it was really more fantasy than science fiction.<sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>2006 Best Short Story for "Sunbird"<sup id="cite_ref-locus_168-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-locus-168">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>2010 Best Short Story for <i>An Invocation of Incuriosity</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-locus_168-11" class="reference"><a The Black Mountains</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-locus_168-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-locus-168">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup> published in <i>Stories</i><sup of the Lane</i></li></ul></li>\n<li>From 1991 to 2009, Gaiman won <a href="/wiki/Eisner_Award" title="Eisner Award">Eisner Awards</a> in the following categories:\n<ul><li>1991 Best Continuing Series: <i>Sandman</i>, by Neil Gaiman and various artists (DC)<sup id="cite_ref-Eisner1990s_176-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eisner1990s-176">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>1991 Best Graphic href="#cite_note-Eisner1990s-176">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>1992 Best Single Issue or Story: <i>Sandman</i> #22-#28: "Season of Mists," by Neil Gaiman and various artists (DC)<sup (DC)<sup id="cite_ref-Eisner1990s_176-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eisner1990s-176">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>1992 Best Writer: Neil Gaiman, <i>Sandman</i>, <i>Books of Magic</i> (DC), <i>Miracleman</i> (Eclipse)<sup id="cite_ref-Eisner1990s_176-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eisner1990s-176">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>1993 Best Continuing Series: <i>Sandman</i> by Neil Gaiman and various artists (DC)<sup id="cite_ref-Eisner1990s_176-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eisner1990s-176">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>1993 Best Graphic Hunters</i>, by Neil Gaiman and Yoshitaka Amano (DC/Vertigo)<sup id="cite_ref-Eisner2000s_177-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eisner2000s-177">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>2004 Best Short Story: "Death," by Neil Gaiman and P. Craig Russell, in <i>The Sandman: Endless Nights</i> (Vertigo/DC)<sup id="cite_ref-Eisner2000s_177-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eisner2000s-177">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>2004 Best Anthology: <i>The Sandman: Endless Nights</i>, by Neil Gaiman and others, edited by Karen Berger and Shelly Bond (Vertigo/DC)<sup id="cite_ref-Eisner2000s_177-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eisner2000s-177">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>2007 Best Archival Collection/Project\xe2\x80\x93Comic Books: <i>Absolute Sandman</i>, vol. 1, by Neil Gaiman and various (Vertigo/DC)<sup id="cite_ref-Eisner2000s_177-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eisner2000s-177">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>2009 Best Publication for Teens/Tweens: <i>Coraline</i>, by Neil Gaiman, adapted by P. Craig Russell (HarperCollins Children\xe2\x80\x99s Books)<sup id="cite_ref-Eisner2000s_177-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eisner2000s-177">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup></li></ul></li>\n<li>In 1991, Gaiman received an <a href="/wiki/Inkpot_Award" title="Inkpot Award">Inkpot Award</a> at the <a href="/wiki/San_Diego_Comic-Con" title="San Diego Comic-Con">San Diego Comic-Con International</a><sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>From 2000 to 2004, Gaiman won <a href="/wiki/Bram_Stoker_Award" title="Bram Stoker Award">Bram Stoker Awards</a> in the following categories:\n<ul><li>2000 Best Illustrated Narrative for <i><a href="/wiki/The_Sandman:_The_Dream_Hunters" title="The Sandman: The Dream Hunters">The class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-locus-168">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>2003 Best Work for Young Readers for <i>Coraline</i><sup id="cite_ref-locus_168-16" class="reference"><a
